It was founded In 1635 by Esnambac and
is one of the most picturesque cities In

dens are among the finest in the world.
It lies. on a .bight of the northwest coast

Pelee and the three-crested Cabret come
together at the sea. The warehouses and
factories He on the small piece of flat
ground next to the sea. while the .resi
dences are up on the hillsides. It has no
harbors, properly speaking, but the road
stead is well protected by the island itself
except on the west and southwest. The
country around is very populous, as is in
fact the whole island, on . some parts of
which there are more - people to . the
square mile than even in the thickly set
tled industrial centers of ' England ; or
Belgium.

Sergeant Manning placed the first oc

Sergeant Dube said that while doing
duty at Jaro, In the Island of Panay, he
conveyed a native, apparently of wealth
and education, to the house of Lieuten
ant Conger. Eighteenth Infantry, where
the lieutenant. Major Glenn and three

without any cause. The witness described
the burning of houses during a march
across Panay and told of the act of their
native guide In cutting off the head of a
Filipino with a bolo before the troops
could get him to surrender.
WITHOUT ANY CAUSE.
January H. Manning of Boston, of the
same regiment, said the water cure was
administered to natives by order of Cap
tain Gregg to secure a confession as to
the death of Private O'Hearne, who was
burned to death. He said there was no
doubt of the guilt of the parties. He also
testified to the kind treatment accorded
to Filipino prisoners.
Isador II. Dube of Watertown. Mass..
formerly sergeant in Company A, Twen
ty-sisth Volunteer Infantry, testified be
fore the Philippine Committee to-day. He
saw the water cure administered to a
native in the presence of Lieutenant Con
cer and Captain Glenn. He also told of
the burning of houses in the Island of Pa
rtay. He cited a case where a woman
â¢was put into a house with fifteen male
prisoners. He said the Filipino prisoners
and the peaceful natives were treated
with great kindness by the Americans.
The verdict of the Smith court-martial
has not yet been received. No one doubts
but it will be acquittal. It is pointed out
that the court would hardly convict Gen
eral Smith lor conducting a campaign that
had received the approval of the Secre
tary of War.
WILL BE ACQUITTED.
In the Senate there has been no dispo
sition so far to justify the "kill and
bum" order of General Smith. On the
contrary. Republican members of that
body have severely criticized it and have
stated that the abuses were not approved
by the administration.
In some quarters the disposition is to
believe that the Secretary of War is so
dissatisfied with the course of events that
he may resign. It is pointed out that he
â¢would hardly care to remain at the head
of the department if Congress should re
fuse to indorse his approval of the Bell-
Smith orders.

ARDMORE, :I. T.; , May 8.-Completion
of the Hartshorne'-Ardmore extension of
the Choctaw, -.Oklahoma and Gulf road
was made to-day. ; This morning at 9.
o'clock the last spike was driven at Dur
wood, when two forces of track-layers,
met. The extension is 116 miles long and;
passes through the central portion of
Chickasaw Nation,* tapping a fine coal re- !
gion. The 'Choctaw was recently pur
chased by the Rock Island road and will;
probably build from Ardmore westward.'
Vice President Holden of the road says
that regular. train service will be put on:
in a few days. This road has railroad:
connection .with Memphis and a direct i
line to the East. '

Malcolm Ford leaves a son about 7 years
old.
Malcolm W. Ford was born in Brook
lyn, February 7, 1867. He was educated
at the Brooklyn Polytechnic Institute. He
took up athletics while at school, and for
a number of years won championships in
this country and Canada, and, in the 100
yard run, /220-yard run and quarter-mile
run, as well as the 120-yard, hurdles, run
ning broad and running high jumps. He
Â¦married a daughter of Robert Graves, a
wall-paper manufacturer, but they were
divorced. .,
Paul Leicester Ford was 37 years old
and a novelist of much note, "Janice
Meredith" and "The Honorable Peter
Stirling" being his most popular novels.-
He was married about a year ago to Miss
Grace Kidder, daughter of Edward H.
Kidder. of Brooklyn. . He was the son of
Gordon Lester Ford of Brooklyn, who
died eleven years- ago and left an estate
of some $2,000,000, divided among his
children, with the exception of Malcolm,
who was disinherited by the will. ,
CAREERS OF THE FORDS.
Malcolm Ford had for some time been
causing uneasiness to the family through
evidences of an unbalanced mind, and de
clared that in his opinion he attacked his
brother during a period of temporary in
sanity. .
"The cause of Malcolm Ford's action,"
said Captain Brown, "can be explained
by inference. For some time past his
financial affairs have been pressing him,
and he had repeatedly asked and received
material, assistance from his brother. It
is presumed that his importuning had
been such, and his demands so excessive,
that his brother had been forced to re
fuse further aid." j

BODY IS OVERLOOKED.
The bullet which killed Paul Ford just
grazed the top of his heart and passed
through a large artery ascending from it.
The bullet which killed Malcolm Ford
was fired with the same accuracy of aim,
for It made a wound scarcely an inch
lower than the one which killed his
brother. In fact, the wounds were al
most identical.

helped him. to a sofa and then ran next

man, still conscious, was carried up to a
room beside his wife's and placed upon
nis bed. â¢ He spoke to his wife and aEked

he expected death and was going to meet
it calmly and bravely. A few moments
later, about twenty minutes after he was
shot, Mr. Ford died.

OPEN'S AUSPICIOUSLY
King and Queen Occupy Koyal Box
for the First Time Since Their
Accession.
LONDON, May 8.â Rarely has the opera
season opened more brilliantly or under
more auspicious circumstances. This
year's opening night will always be re
membered by the fact that It was the flr3t
night that the King and Queen had occu*
pied the royal box since their accession.
There was one unfortunate circum
stance in that the roadway authorities
still had Bow street torn up, though tfiey
had plenty of time and notice to get the
work finished before such a great night.
Much confusion of carriages was caused
in the approach to the opera-house. Â¦ So
great indeed was the block that even the
royal carriage with the King and Queen
could not get by, and in conaequence the
curtain had to be kept down fully ten
minutes to allow the King and Queen to
get in before the playing of the national
anthem, when the whole great audience
rose and cheered with more vigor than
â¢was ever before heard in the opera-house.
It was a most impressive sight. The
Queen was looking radiantly beautiful in
a black dress With a small diamond tiarti.
The Princess Victoria and Princess
Charles of Denmark, who accompanied
their father and mother, were very sim
ply dressed in white.

Bedding 1 Man Has a Costly Experi
ence, but Saves His Diamond
Ring.
REDDING, May 8.-Antone Jaegel, a
saloonman and large j property owner â¢ of
this c!ty. lay in bed at an early hour this
morning and watched a burglar coolly
ransack drawers and gather up jewelrv
and money belonging to himself, his wife
and his young daughter.
Jaegel was awakened by a faint' noise
and was dropping to sleep again when a
fresh sound roused him and he opened
his eyes. By the faint light ln- the room
he saw a tall burglar at work at the
bureau. Jaegel watched him pocket hia
I wife s pin money, cut open a satchel con
taining his daughter's coin, pocket a pair
of opera glasses and then reach for
Jaegel's JoO gold watch.
The man in bed was unarmed and re
frained-from making a sound for fear
the burglar would discover the $500 dia
mond ring upon his finger and murder
him for It- He could not, however, see
the watch go without a protest, and he
coughed. The burglar took instant alarm,
dropped the watch, ran out into the hall
of the upstairs flat, called his partner,
who was on guard, and ran down the rear
stairs. Jaegel ran down the front stair
way and summoned help, but the burg
lars escaped and have not been appre
hended.

The banquet at the pavilion to-night
was an elaborate and brilliant affair.
Fully 1000 people enjoyed the repast. Su
preme Sub-Chief Rangrer C. P. Rendon of
Stockton was the toastmaster. ! During
the evening he presented, on behalf of the
delegates, to retiring Grand President
Heenan a solid gold watch with diamond
studded fob and locket with the emblem
upon It. Heenan was also presented a
silver match safe with the emblem. Stock
ton Circle, Companions of the Forest, pre
sented the Grand Court a silver-mounted
gavel Chief Companion Ella Halley mak
ing the presentation.
The grand chief ranger was authorized
by resolution to appoint a committee of
three to revise the laws governing subor
dinate courts and report at the next Grand
Court meeting in Santa Cruz. The relief
board was granted Â«^ cents out of the
per capita tax fii the 17,901 members in
the State April 1. The printing of the
laws in the Italian language was ordered.
A recess was then taken to to-morrow
morning.
Most of the afternoon session was taken
up with the consideration of amendments
to the constitution. Thirty amendments
were adopted, eleven rejected and one re
ferred back. Of the adopted resolutions
there are but two of general public In
terest. One allows mileage to all past
grand chief rangers in actual attendance
upon the Grand Court. A second makes it
impossible for a sick member .to become
unfinancial, the amount of the dues being
added to his sick benefits.
under one charter. On the statement of
the treasurer of the court that they would
affiliate harmoniously, he withdrew the
amendment, and the report of the com
mittee was adopted unanimously amid
much enthusiasm.
During the afternoon session of the
Grand Court, the committee appointed to
draft resolutions touching upon the as
sassination of the late President McKin
ley presented a report, which was adopted
unanimously.

APPEARANCE OF ONE OF
HANFORD'S STREETS DUR
ING THE ENCAMPMENT.
YATES VICTORY
A DECISIVE ONE
Major Hawley was born in England
June 16, 1846, but came to the United
States with his parents in 1860. Respond
ing to President Lincoln's call for troops
he enlisted in Company A. Twenty
eighth Wisconsin Infantry, and served
with that regiment In the Mobile cam
paign, taking part in the engagements
of Fort Blakeley and Spanish Fort. Haw
ley was with the first troops to enter Mo
bile. Subsequently his company was or
dered to Brazos de Santiago, Texas, and
from, there to Clarksville and Browns
ville, upon the Rio Grande, when the
French troops were ordered out of, Mex
ico by President- Lincoln. :/. Hawley . was
discharged at Brownsville August -, 18,
1865, and arrived at Madison, Wis,,' in
September, 1865, where the troops were
mustered out. The Twenty-eighth Wis
consin Regiment was commanded by
Colonel E. B. Gray, the late . adjutant
general of the Grand Army of the Re
public. Hawley served in the campaigns
under Generals Clayton. Benton, Gran
ger, Reynolds, Canby and Sheridan.

as follows: : Â¦ -
The men in the tunnel say that there
was a rush and a roar and then all was
over. The mules, twenty 'in number, were
swept off their feet by the first edge oi
the slide and later in the day only scraps
of their remains could be found. Tne
mine buildings, engine-house cookhousr.
bunkhouse and other buildings all went
down with a series of crashes. Â¦ "Where
they stood Is now covered with a blanket
of crushed ice from the glacier, which
came in the slide, and broken splinters of
trees. The loss in buildings and damage
will be about $6000. All day men worked
excavating the debris and endeavoring to
find the bodies of the two missing- men.
but at a late hour to-night neither had
been recovered. The debris is thirty feet
in death in some places where the slide
piled itself up in the valley below. This
is the most serious slide in the Kootenai
this spring. ' -
Powis* pack train had Just arrived 'at
the mine from Kasklo. The mules were
ranged in front of the buildings, having
their, packs removed, when one of the
workmen noticed far above the swaying
of trees, which gave evidence of the rusn
of the slide. The men ran in a body for
the mouth of the open tunnel and were
not a moment too soon. Two fell be
hind and were lost in the disaster, j They
were John Douglas, the mine blacksmith,
and another man whose name ia not
given. Â¦ "
The slide occurred at 11 o'clock. A shelf
of rock with a glacier of ice many feet
In depth came tumbling down "Washing
ton Mountain and everything in ita path
above ground was carried hundreds of
yards | down the mountain and buried.
Mosi of the men around the camp saw
the slide start from the mountain sido
above them and rushed into the mouth of
a tunnel, else the death list would have
been ten. times as great as It was. ' r â¢ '

First Spike of a New Bailway.
ANTHONY, Kans.,' May 8.â The first
spike of the Kansas, Mexico and "Orient
Railway in the United States was driven
here to-day. The first rails of the track
that is to reach from Kansas City to tho
west coast of Mexico were spiked down
hard and ; fast to-day and the work of
construction , thus begun will go steadily
on as fast as men'can build It The road
bed is graded from here to a point 300
miles southwest. Officers of the road
estimate that In eighteen months or two
years the road will be completely built
to the coast. *. s J~

FUROR AT ELECTION.
The scene of the election this morning
was undoubtedly the most sensational in
the history, of the federation. The crowd
that at three biennials have clamored to
have Mrs. Platt-Decker of Colorado as
their leader were simply set wild at the
mention of her name by Mrs. Gallagher
of . Ohio, who : made the -nominating
speech, and the intense excitement was
as an electric current as it passed
through the audience. Had Mrs. \ Decker
yielded one iota from the firm position
she has taken from the beginning that
as a matter, of justice it should go to
Mrs. Denison, who for one year has been
acting president instead of. Mrs. Lowe,
she could easily have stampeded the
house. . As it was she arose in the gallery
where she sat with the Colorado delega
tion, and with an- earnestness and
straightforwardness not to be mistaken
declined the ! honor. ) '
In a hjimorous way Mrs. Lowe refused
to have Mrs. Decker's name taken from
the blackboard, where it had been placed
by the nominating committee, and,. after
witty passes between the two,' Mrs.
Decker came down to the floor and made
almost a fierce demand that her name be
removed. The dramatic side had reached
the danger line, â¢ whereupon 'Mrs. -Lowe
recommended to. the convention that it
be erased. Though this was done many
of her admirers voted for her anyway,
the vote standing 667 for Mrs. Denison
and 258 for Mrs. Decker^ - â¢
-Though the Decker â¢ forces were keenly
disappointed they have with charming
grace gone to the support of Mrs. Deni
8on - -the girl president," as some one
called her, .-for she is a slight, youthful
woman yet in. the thirties. Mrs. Denison
came to the convention worn out," â¢ and
this morning was -. slightly ill, but there
â¢s no doubt In the minds of the leaders
that she will be equa) to the high of
nce, having had . training as president
four years. of the famous Sorosis of New
York, a/ position she was filling at the
time jjf her election to the higher of
fice this morning. -When called upon for
a speech she v said in beginning: -
In this beautiful City of the Angels
you have placed upon me the royal robes.
I can only say. 'I thank you.'"

PRAISE FOR FEDERATION.
Two years ago, in summing up my labors as
president of , this organization, I undertook to
give you some idea of its growth in member
ship. As we closed the first decade of our
organized efforts it seemed to me that the most
Impressive fact of our association was this
gain Â¦ In numerical strength. And to-night
when we reflect upon the humble beginnings
from which" has developed . this organization
numbering its hundreds of thousands of
women we' find it somewhat difficult to realize
the enormous proportions we- have attained.
But, . my friends, I am not- so much con
cerned with telling you to-night how many .we
have gained in numbers in the past' two years
as I am to "tell you what these numbers have
accomplished. For, after all, the salient and
most hopeful feature of our organization lies
not In our numerical strength, â¢ which has en
abled us and will enable us not only: to think
things but do things, and do things- not only
tir Improve and uplift ourselves,/ but to render
important service in the uplifting | and better
ment or humanity. To this end,' although our
size, numbers and material success are indeed
Important factors, yet . we wish to. have our
material success Inspired with a recognition
oÂ£ spiritual values as well. It is time that
we more fully comprehend that . we are no
longer In the preliminary and adolescent stage
of existence. The question which Interests us
Is no longer the Increase of numbers, for j the
General Federation of Women's Clubs has
"come of age." . , Â» '
. j The agitation and unrest . pervading our . or
ganization Â«eem to me to be" signs . of growth
and health. It is by means of such experiences
that" we are finding our soul. We cannot form
any' adequate conception of the vast changes
that will be brought about as j women become
more and more self-conscious. I use the term
in its deep sense and mean that as women be
come more and more conscious of their powers
and the possibilities of their souls they will in
evitably demand wider â¢ fields for their activi
ties, and that with the wider play of their ac
tivities-will come increased responsibilities. -
The glory- of â¢ motherhood will receive a new
luster by the recognition of .what â¢ motherhood
means to civilization, v Even a superficial glance
at the development , of " society shows that
woman by virtue of her; maternity has -.been
the -conscrver of the human race. > Ever, hedged
in as she has been, a; a rule, she has. been
and Is. a constructive agent.- As her experience
oi life becomes more extende'd she will continue
to be a constructive agent, but no longer In
the restrictive sense. With unerring vision 6he
will . see that her; obligations. :. as mother, > are
without limit.- In order that her children may
have the best possible surroundings physically,
mentally Â¦ and. morally she, : will begin to under
stand.that it is her business to help and^bet
ter every condition to which her children may
be exposed. .As , she comprehends more and
more the .' needs â¢ of her own- children she â¢ also
comprehends the .needs | of other- people's 'chil
dren. -With the income of understanding and
partners of those on the speclar train will
enviously dream of the pretty girls,
orange groves, deciduous orchards, * vine-

to make life in the great San Joaquin

Special Dispatch to The Call.
CHICAGO, May 8.â The injunction- bill
which United States Attorney Bethea is
preparing to file against the beef trust
has struck a snag somewhere, and may
not reach the Chicago Federal Court be
fore Monday of next week. All prepara
tions had been made to file the bill to
morrow, and- word had been received
from Washington that it had been for
warded to Chicago so it would reach here
early this evening. It Is now intimated,
however, that nothing might be done De
fore next week. /
It was learned to-day from an authori- i
tative source that the Government hopes
to make -an injunction stick against
members of the combine on two mate
rial clauses In it which deal with the
agreement of the -packers to maintain a
standard price, and the rebates from the
railways. It developed, however, that
not one specific case which the Govern
ment can prove against the packers lead,
ing to any criminal practice or possible
later indictment by the Federal Grand
Jury exists, and it was further ascer
tained that most of the Government wit-*
nesses will be stenographers and ex
employes. Its star witness is said to be
a discredited employe, who now bears an
assumed name.
According to a report which comes
from a source near to headquarters the
Government will try to prove that the
packers are maintaining prices under an
agreement made in 1SS9 which provides
for a fine of $5000 for its violation. The
lowest rate fixed in the agreement is said
to be so- high that a. good profit is as
sured. A previous agreement, known as
the agreement of 1887, is said to .have as
sessed a fine of only $1000.
PACKERS TO BE CALLED.
Irt connection with these agreements
and reported secret meetings of pack
ers to arrange prices it was semi-officially
announced that Frank Vogel of Nelson.
Morris & Co., P. A. Valentine of Ar
mour & Co., and Louis F. Swift of Swift
& Co.. would be called as witnesses by
the Government. From another source
it was learned that It was the hope of
the prosecution by calling these men to
strengthen the accusations made by for
mer stenographers and confidential men
in the packers' employ.
As to any criminal prosecution an offi
cial of the Government is authority for
the information that under the bill for
equity such as the bill United States At
torney Bethea will file, the packers could
even come into court and freely admit
they had been parties to a trust or com
bine, and according to the constitution
evidence so admitted could not be used
against them in a criminal prosecution.
For that reason, said the same official,
thfi entire investigation of the beef ques
tion and filing of the bill of restraint can
ena only in the packers possibly .admit
ting .that they have In ways acted in re
straint of trade, but "would be good after
this and would not do so any more." and
the Government will be powerless to pun
ish them.
A well-known attorney in speaking of
the coming investigation and its outcome
said: "What has there been mentioned in
connection with the methods of the pack
ers that is in any way the basis for an
indictment? Absolutely nothing. There is
not an injunction that a living man can
draw up. an act of Congress or any ac
tion of court that can affect the price of
livestock and beef an eighth of a cent
other than it is being affected by the law
of supply and demand. How is the Gov
ernment going to show that the packers
have combined or conspired by extortion
or fraud to force prices? The Federal au
thorities will certainly ; have to do that
before any indictment will stick. The talk
about securing evidence to place before
the Federal Grand Jurv or of going-be
fore that body and indicting the leading
men in the business is nonsense."
GRAND JUEY ACTS.
ST. LOUIS, May 8.â The beef trust is
now under Investigation by the St. Louis
Grand Jury. Following the revelation at
Jefferson City that diseased meat is sold
in St. Louis. Circuit Attorney Folk to-day
presented the subject to the Grand Jury
He announces that he will subpena all
the witnesses who testified at Jefferson
City and all other persons whom he can
find that know anything: about the opera
tions of the beef companies.
"This has become a subiect for the
criminal courts," said Folk, "and the
matter will be as thoroughly Investigated
a- have been the bribery scandals, and I
have no doubt that men who have been
Belling diseased meat to St. Loulsans will
be landed behind the bars of the peni
tentiary." .

.Morgans bind themselves to give Har
land & Wolff all orders for new vessels

In return Harland & Wolff agree to
build no ships for any other firm but

burg-American line, provided the orders
of the combine keep the builders' works
fully and continuously employed.

trolled or directed by English law."

BUTTE, Mont., May 8.â A special to the
Miner from Anaconda says that nine men
were severely injured, one fatally, in an
explosion to-day of a ladle containing five
tons of molten copper in the converter of
the "Washoe smelter. The accident oc
curred at the noon hour, when the big
ladle was being emptied, and is supposed
to have been caused by cold water com
ing in contact with the metal. The
molten copper was hurled with terriflo
force, and had the accident occurred dur
ing the working hours the list of casual
ties would have been considerable.
Larry Dower, who it la believed will
die, was frightfully mangled.

The Republican Primary League has a
score to settle with Gage. There Is no
doubt as to the anti-Gage attitude of the
League. The fact is well remembered that
Gasre projected himself into a local battle
to defeat this organization in a primary
election. Special care will be taken in
the next contest to eliminate Gageites
from the League tickets. If Gage has any
<*oubt concerning the temper of the Re
publican Primary League let him write a
fctter to the organization. j
Senator Perkins is in a bad fix. His al
liance with the bosses In the fight over
the Collectorshlp of Internal Revenue
threatens to cause him endless trouble.

its of pollt Ical sagacity when he advised
George Hatton to follow Collector John
Lyncb's advice in the selection of candi
dates for the Legislature. In making this
suggestion to Hatton the Senator snowed
but little respect for the, order of the
President against Interference of Federal
officeholders in political affairs. Con
trary to the spirit and letter of President
Sooeevelt's order, Lynch is doing politics
PERKINS IN BAD FIX.
For some reason best known to himself
Governor Gage has seen fit to treat the
Union League Club with scant courtesy.
He did not make up his mind to attend
the League reception in honor of Senator
Bard until the eleventh hour, and then
rave as a reason for his peculiar conduct
H statement that he had not received a
formal invitation. The real facts are th-it
the club sent a special committee to Sac
ramento to personally invite him. The
committee saw the Governor and invited
him. The club members took into ac
count Gage's chagrin over the defeat ot
Burns and the election of Bard and over
Â°The Unfon League Club has no candi
date for Governor. At the next dinner E.
F. Prerton and Dr. Pardee may be placed
on the list of speakers. Beforethe Re
publican convention assembled! homas
Flint. E. B. Edson, Frank Short and W.
iX. Cutter may have ample opportunity to
address their fellow members of the
League. Gage may regard such speaking
as a piece of effrontery on the part of
the gubernatorial candidates and conclude
to write a- letter expressive of his dis
pleasure. It may please his Gageshlp to
remind the Union League that he expects
a renomtnation. No doubt the League
would imitate the example of the San
Jose convention and allow such a letter
to be read.
GOVEBNOB IS INVITED.
The Union League Club of San Fran
cisco proposes a banquet or reception in
honor of Governor Odell of New York.
The Governor is not sure at this time that
his engagements will allow him oppor
tunity to attend a club function, but he
will know when he ar*iyes In San Fran
cisco r.ext Sunday whether he can accept.
The statement that invitations haye been
issued to the proposed banquet is prema
ture. No one has been invited and no one
has been "turned down." Guests will not
be â¢ invited until Governor Odell signifies
his acceptance.
The Union League is. an Institution
maintained by Republicans for the pur
pose of supporting Republican principles
and promoting party success. Its mem
bership of 500 embraces many active and
influential Californiansâ men who have
achieved distinction for energy and enter
prise. It is the custom of the League to
extend its hospitality to eminent repre
sentatives of the Republican party. Pres
idents Harrison and McKinley, Governor
>ash of Ohio, Cabinet officers. United
States Senators and others holding high
station in public life have been the guests
of the League. The Mayor of San Fran
cisco, a progressive Republican, was re
cently the honored guest of the club. The
Mayor's eloquent tribute to the principles
of 'human liberty produced a profound
impression.

SAN DOMINGO, Santo Domingo,
May 8.â The United States Min
ister, William F. Powell, owing to
the situation of affairs here, has
suspended all intercourse with the
revolutionary government and is prepar
ing to leave San Domingo for Hayti, to
which country he Is also accredited. His
departure is due to the grave difficulties
existing In Hayti. where a revolution Is
said to be in progress.
ostensibly for Perkins. Later on the
bosses may attempt to deliver the goods
to some candidate other than Perkins.
The Senator is not wholly satisfied with
Hatton's work under Lynch' s direction.
He has other agents In the field. The in
dependent agencies have given warning
of the danger that threatens ' the senior
Senator. There is no telling what will
happen in California next November. The
spirit of independence Is abroad. In the
land. All signs point to a popular upris
ing against bosses and all those affiliating
with bossism. The people will have op
portunity to elect 100 members of the Leg
islature. Only sixty-one members arc
needed to elect a United States Senator.
In accordance with a decree of the Chief
of the Revolution, the laws passed and
the decrees issued by competent legal au
thorities are to remain In force until the
promulgation of the new constitution.
A number of political ' exiles have al

_ The shock of the earthquake was felt at
Souscnate. republic of Salvador, more than 500
miles away." â . Â¦ - , ..,
I was governess for the family of J. Apariclo,
who is a gentleman,' of much wealth and the
owner of much' property In the city of Quezal
temango. I was living at his home, which is
the finest residence in the city, being a beauti
ful three-story -structure. Like - most other
houses it has Its main entrance in the- center
of the house, but there was a side entrance
built out like an alcove. I afterward learned
the reason for having this side entrance.
We were nil inside nt. the time of the earth
quake and when It occurred I. made a rush for
the front door, but Mr. Aparlcio, taking me by
the arm, said: "Don't go out that door, or you
will sorely be killed." He then hurried me to
the side entrance with the others. Just as we
entered the side alcovo the house fell In, and
If I had been at the front door, for which I
had . started, . I surely would have been killed,
but as it was we all found ourselves perfectly
safe, as the heavy framework of the alcove
had withstood the shock. I was Informed that
these alcoves or side entrances were built es
pecially strong, and it was the custom in case
ot earthquakes, to stand underneath them. ThÂ»
entire town was destroyed and more than 500
persons lost their lives.

The committee administered a rebuke to
the Board of Works by ordering the or
dinance reducing the . width of sidewalks
on Anthony street to ten feet to be amend-\
ed so that they will be restored to their
original width of thirteen feet. A. Aron
son complained that "he had bep n ordered
to tear out some underground work In his
building on the street named, although he
had been Informed by the board that the
sidewalks were thirteen feet wide. The
board subsequently Informed him that the
width had been reduced and the changes
would cost him $5000.
The Board of Works was authorized to
advertise for bids for the repair of bitu
minous pavements, at an estimated cost
of $20,000, and to pave Howard street be
tween Twenty-fifth and Twenty-sixth
streets.
J. T. Burke, representing the Southern
Pacific Company, informed the committee
that the corporation was desirous of hav
ing its engineers explain the route for
which a franchise has been asked for a
cut-off line from Sunnydale avenue to
Sixth street, and also to give information
as to the intended method of construc
tion. Consideration of the franchise was
postponed for one week to hear from the
residents of the Mission district as. to the
advisability of granting a franchise
through the Mission on the existing route.
Charles Wheeler appeared before the
committee in support of the petition of
property owners for. the opening of Lyon
street. Mr. Wheeler said that the estab
lished law is that Lyon street Is eighty
feet wide north of Union street, and per
sons occupying the street are obstruction
ists and should be removed. He advised
the city to purchase the so-called Miran
da grant, made in 1833 by the Mexican
Government, and tb make an appropria
tion in the budget for the purpose. The
committee decided to notify parties ob
structing the streets to appear before the
joint Committee on Judiciary and Streets
on May 16.
The Board of Works submitted a plat
showing obstructions on Lyon street. The
City Engineer says there has been some
contention as to the proper location of
Lyon street, and the question may again
be raised. It has been contended .that there
should be no deflection, but the deflected
â¢line has in recent Supreme Court deci
slonp been held to be correct. "

last namd declined to accept either offer.

The following is given as ' a method to
destroy green lice and, other Insects on
rose bushes and other plants without in
jury to the plants: "Take of quassia chips
314 ounces, . larkspur seed 5 drams; boil
these together in 7 pints of water till the
decoction is reduced to 5 pints. When the
liquid is cooled it is to be strained and
used,with a watering pot or bulb syringe,
as may 1 be most convenient, preferably
the latter,' as -by its use all affected parts
can be reached."

CENTERS OF POPULATIONâ A. S.
R., Sacramento, Cal. The center of pop
ulation In the United States in 1850 was
twenty-three miles southeast of Parkers
burg, in the present. State of West Vir
ginia; in 1860 twenty miles south of Chll
licothe, Ohio; 1S70 forty-eight miles north
of Cincinnati; Ohio; 1880 eight miles west
by south of Cincinnati, Ohio; 1890 twenty
miles east of Columbus, Ind.; 1900 six
miles southeast of Columbus, Ind. '
; .One>pf the oddities of,, the time 'is; a ruction, in

Intensive gardening. enables skillful teachers" to
perform marvels with the small plats,; of ground at
their disposal. Trees as well as 'shrubs. and ,- small
plants are grown in the narrowest grounds, and. the
writer for the Transcript says : : ." "They "are ' .' often
grown in stocks and are dwarf, both roots and
branches having severe pruningrevery year. .Some
times .the teacher owns the! trees and" carries them
.with him \ when " appointed to a new : school;- ; Sorhe-
In the cities, of course, the gardens are small. In
some cases they. do not exceed one-tenth of an acre,
but in such cases the cities provide larger central gar
dens from which specimens of plants are distributed
to the schools arid which are frequently visited by. the
pupils. Thus in Berlin there is a central garden of
ten acres, while at Breslau there is a combined gar
den and forest of 12,000 acres for school use: <
France, which by reason of her soil and her cli
mate is the ideal land of gardens, leads the van with
more than 30,000 school gardens in operation. Semi
barbarous Russia has upward ,of 10,000 village
school gardens. Cold and bleak Sweden has more
than 5000, and hardly a rural school in that king
dom is' without one. Switzerland does nearly as well
as Sweden, and _it is estimated that throughout
Europe there "are at this time more than 100,000 such
gardens,' not counting kindergartens.
By way of contributing information on the subject
the Boston Transcript has begun the publication of
a series of articles on the school gardens of Europe
and America. It appears that such gardens were es
tablished in Europe fully a hundred years ago, but
were originally directed and maintained by the own
ers of large estates, who wished to teach their ten
ants how to make the highest profit out of their
small bits of ground. When the benefits of the sys
tem were proven the Governments took up the work,
and now in every European country except Great
Britain there are extensive systems of school gar
dens, not only in the rural districts, but in the cities
as well.
WITH the coming of spring our Eastern
fellow countrymen, released from the bond
age of a hard winter, are taking a keen de
light in every form of plant that can be made. to bud
and blossom in their cities. As a part of that delight
they are interesting themselves in the establishment
of school gardens. It seems altogether probable
that as a result of the enthusiasm ton the subject
every progressive city in the East will, in a com
paratively short time have well managed gardens
of that kind. '' .. _ \ Â¦
EUROPEAN SCHOOL GARDENS.
After many futile efforts to devise a
method of classifying warships by nu
merical figures or value an approach to
success has been made. The strong point
In favor of the system proposed is that
the values assigned to a considerable
number of vessels under the rule laid
down correspond almost exactly with the
values given, by the foremost naval ex
perts. The new method is difficult of non
technical explanation, but some of the
results established by It are easily under
stood. The highest figure of merit is that
of the New Jersey class, with a figure of
116. Next come the British King Edward
and the Italian Benedetto Brin with. 104
the French Republlque with 94 and the
Russian Prince Potemklne with 98. The
second-class battleships of all powers cut
a very poor figure and stand, as a rule,
below the modern armored crulgers.â
Chicago News.
Ne-w Flan of Classifying Warships.

DEBATESâ J. M. E., City. This depart
ment has on a number of occasions an
nounced that it does not furnish reasons
to be' Â¦used on either side; of a debate.
The idea of a debate is that the party
who takes part in it shall furnish his
own reasons for the position he takes,
and not to advance the ideas of others.
HEN AND CHICKENâ A. S. F., Ar
mona, Kings County, Cal. A chicken is
the young of fowl,, particularly of the do
mestic hen. Hen is the female of any
kind of fowl, but particularly of the do
mestic fowl, or, as, it is sometimes called,
the barnyard fowl: One two years old
can hardly be considered a chicken.

Dust as a cause of pulmonary tubercu
losis, according to the report of the Im
perial Health Office at Berlin, Is clearly
shown in the statistics of the patients

It is a good sign that Democratic Senators leave
the Senate when Tillman speaks. Perhaps the time
is not far distant when even South Carolina Democ
racy will turn its back on. him, and then the black
guard will cease to disgrace the Senate by his pres
ence as he offends it by his insoknea-
It seems to have been the intention of Tillman in
making the speech to convey the impression that the
Republican administration is treating the Filipinos
worse than the Democrats of South Carolina have
treated the negroes. Perhaps it is natural for him
to think so. He is himself so bitter and venomous
in his hatred of the negro that he cannot believe any
white man has respect for the rights of the colored
races. As Senator Burton said in replying to him:
"A man who upon this floor can defend slavery and
government by the shotgun cannot be expected to
think very well of Senators who arc trying to' carry
good government into the Philippines. It is astound
ing that /a Senator should make an appeal for hon
cst government in one breath and in the next boast
of crimes almost unparalleled in history." ;
Tillman felt the force of the retort and at once
burst forth in his old harangue against the negro.'
Once more he told how the negro vote in South
Carolina was suppressed and is still held in suppres
sion, and once more he virtually challenged Republi
cans to provide a remedy for the wrong. . He re
peated the story of the shotgun campaign and said
the whites had carried the election by 3000 majority
and might just as well have made it 30,000. Refer
ring to the so-called "sand cure" practiced by Amer
icans upon recalcitrant Filipinos, he said it is but
a mild method and added: "When we get ready to
put a nigger's face in the sand we put his body there,
too."
Senator McComas, in reply to an attack of Til
man and Money of Mississippi upon tÂ£e Philippine
policy of the administration, said: "We behold Sen
ators from the two States where there is less popu
lar liberty than in the. others shouting loudest for
constitutional liberty on the other side of the globe."
The statement was a fair hit. That wrong has been
done to negroes in Mississippi and South Carolina
does not of course justify wrong in the Philippines,
but it does furnish good reason' why those whp profit
by the wrong should not undertake to pose before
the American people as the special champions of jus
tice and make pretense of being the . defenders of
liberty against the assaults of better men than them
selves.
j Tillman's boast is that he is no hypocrite â that he
tells the truth. A similar boast might be made by
any blackguard who regards- courtesy as hypocrisy
and modesty as cowardice. He violates decency, and
he calls the violation a display of honesty. He vaunts
and brags of infamy as if it were an honor, and chal
lenges the Senate and the nation to silence him.
In his reply on Wednesday to Senator McComas
of Maryland Tillman became so offensive that we are
told most of the Democratic Senators left the
chamber, being unwilling to give even a tacit recog
nition to him as a representative of their party.
Nor can they.be blamed for doing so. Surely any
one with whom Tillman is associated even remotely
has occasion to be ashamed of it.
SO far from having been abashed by the em
phatic condemnation recently pronounced
upon him by' the Seriate arid ' by the well nigh
universal voice of the American people, Senator
Tillman appears to glory in liis shame. Not for a
single day has he by word or by manner shown any
contrition for the disgraceful attack made upon his
colleague, McLaurin, upon the floor of the Senate,
nor has he exhibited any respect for public senti
ment. On the contrary, he seems to find an ignoble
pleasure in continuing to offend against the rules of
the Senate and the decencies of discussion.

ANSWERS TO QUERIES.*
PERSONAL MENTION.
O-, L. RICHARDS of El Reno, Okla., has what is, perhaps, the most valu
able Indian curio in existence and one which cannot be duplicated In
â¢ any collection > of relics of the: red men. It Is an elk teeth dress.
â¢ Richards has just purchased this garment from Mary Roman Nose,
nee Bent, granddaughter of the famous Colonel Bent and wife of a fam
ous Cheyenne brave. It is the most cherished heirloom of the Cheyenne tribÂ«
and has long been the envy of curio hunters in this country and abroad.
For more than a century it has been in the possession of the tribe to which
Mary Roman Nose belongs. Jn fact, it was originally owned by her great-grand
father, who counted It among his treasures. â¢ 'Â« '.
Â¦ It took Richards many months to induce her to close the bargain with him,
which left him $1600 poorer and Mary Roman Nose minus the famous dress. This
robe is ornamented with 1024 elk teeth, mostly gifts from great chiefs. Joseph,
the great Nez Perces chief:. the famous Black Kettle, Chief Red Moon, White
Shield, Stone Calf, Little Raven and a host of other red men who gained fame
for their .tribes on the warpath and in the hunt are represented in this garment.
Each one of these teeth has the chief's mark on it. When it Is remembered
that elks' teeth now bring from. $1 to $10, and that they are very scarce even at
the latter figure, and that to the Indians they represented so much wealth, the
value of this curious costume can be thoroughly appreciated.
Many- romances and a few tragedies are connected with this dress. ! Forty In
dian maidens, robed in the dress, have sworn allegiance to swarthy braves. It Is
held by the Indians that a maiden who becomes a squaw robed in it may feel
sure of holding forever the affection of the brave she weds.
The Agricultural Department estimates that up
ward of .15 per cent of allthe foodstuffs consumed in
this country are adulterated or mislabeled, and that
fully 2 per cent of the frauds are injurious to health.
Since American ingenuity has been cfever enough to
devise such frauds,. American law should be equal
to the task of preventing them.
After that statement Dr. Bigelow made another
which is of special interest to Californians: "It is
not generally known, I think, that it is possible to
import European oils into this country, ship them
across the country by rail and put them on the mar
ket at San Francisco at a lower price than that for
which the first grade of California olive oils can be
made."
Some interesting facts were gathered by Dr. Bige
low of the Bureau of Chemistry concerning the mis
branding of olive oils. In his testimony .before the
committee the doctor said: "The oils marked olive
that are brought into this country are very fre
quently adulterated with peanut and sesame oils.
They are also said to add in foreign countries sun
flower and mustard oils, but \yc have not found them.
We have found only peanut and sesame oils in our
investigations of those 'olive oils.' Here is a label
which recalls a very interesting interview I had with
a clerk in Buffalo. They had two brands of 'olive
oil/ as they were marked. One was American, they
said. The other was imported., They looked very
much alike. I asked the clerk if he could give me a
quart bottle of the imported oil. He said he could
bottle me one. I asked if his firm; bottled it them
selves. He said yes, and finally admitted 'that those
two supplies of oil were bottled from the v same bar
rel and that they were both cottonseed oil. The bot
tle he sold me was labeled 'Olive oil put up by
Freres duPeaux, Bordeaux, France.' "
Some interesting recipes are given for making
fine liquors. Thus if brandy be desired the recipe
says: "To forty gallons of colored French spirits
add two ounces of brandy oil, mix well and add one
quart of white syrup or glycerine. To imitate any
particular brand add. a small quantity of the kind to
be imitated. About three gallons to forty will an
swer the purpose." A recipe for Rhine wine says:
"Mix one pound of essence in three gallons of proof
spirits and add to tliirty-seven\gallons of rectified
cider; then dissolve a pound of 'Â¦ tartaric acid in a
half-gallon of hot water and add to suit taste." The
country is full of the kind of brandy made by that
recipe, and it is said the wine recipe shows us how
is made "about one-half the Rhine wine that is used
in the United States."
It appears from the facts cited by the Senator that
even the cheapest foods are subject -to fraud. Dried
codfish is not a costly article, and yet a still cheaper
fish is put up like it, labeled "choice codfish" and
sold in the market as the genuine article. Some
times the frauds are ingenious. Thus the market is
full of genuine vanilla"" beans from which all the
vanilla has been extracted, and the oil of nutmegs is
extracted by a clever process, the punctures filled
with' lime solution and the nutmegs pat upon the
market again. Apple cores arid peeling are used to
make strawberry jelly because apples are cheaper
than strawberries, but the apple does not escape, for
as pumpkins are cheaper still they are used in making
apple butter.
SENATOR McCUMBER in a recent speech on
the pure food bill now before Congress pre
sented a new array of facts obtained by Gov
ernment experts showing the' extent to which food
adulterations, imitations, and misbranding are prac
ticed by unscrupulous dealers. They reveal also the
difficulty that the producers of honest foods have
in finding a market for. their products in the face
of such illegitimate competition, and as a conse
quence are matters of concern to growers and to le
gitimate manufacturers as well as to consumers, "<, %
FRAUDS IN FOODSTUFFS.

Mrs. Annie Besant, who in the course
of her strenuous life, has been a very
busy â¢writer of books, has now left India
for England, though I hear she means to
return thither in the autumn. Mrs. Be
sant has been living more or less continu
ously at Benares for the last seven years.
She has been very gravely ill from the
fever that hangs around that most un
sanitary neighborhood, but she is now
almost completely recovered.

monthly sections. He has already writ

A postponement of the measure from this- session
to the next would expose the bill to the hazard of
a. still further postponement, for the next session will
be short. - Moreover, the question might then be
come involved in the partisan conflicts of the Presi
dential election. It is of course desirable to avoid
such contingencies. The people have had experience
enough of the evil effects of making money ques
tions a part of the political issues which divide the
people. Prolonged agitations on ' such matters se
riously interfere with commerce and industry and
cause great hardships in many cases. This, then, is
the time to deal with the currency problem, and
strong pressure should be put upon Congress to have
it settled by the adoption of the Fowler bill before
adjournment
The bill has received the cordial indorsement of
the executive committee of the Indianapolis Mone
tary Convention and represents the best thought of
the ablest financial experts of the Union. It offers a
substantial improvement in our financial system, and
its benefits will- affect all classes of people.
The Fowler bill, now before the House, includes
the vital points of the maintenance of the parity of
our metallic currency by redeeming silver coin in
gold and of providing an ample credit currency by
conservative gradual steps. The provisions made in
that respect are in keeping with the pledges of the
Republican party in the Philadelphia platform, and
have met with the approval of financiers in all parts
of the country. As the bill provides for the reduc
tion of denominations of silver certificates gold can
be paid out for silver without requiring any increase
in the gold reserve provided by the law of 1900, and
until that machinery is provided.and the maintenance
of parity secured there will be always more or less
money agitatfon to disturb our financial credit.
We are enjoying such a degree of universal pros
perity at the present time. There is no mo.ney
stringency in any part of the country arid no im
mediate danger of the occurrence of one. As was
said by Secretary Gage shortly before his retire
ment: "Looked at from the point of true states
manship it would appear that the present is the most
propitious hour in all our history and as favorable as
any period we may hope for in the future to enter
upon reasonable and judicious measures to eliminate
all elements of financial weakness which experience
has brought to light and to perfect our now faulty
system 'of currency."
Each of the bills stated is in some respects- a
measure of urgency, for the conditions of com
merce and industry call for them all. There
are, however, special reasons, why the currency bill
should be acted upon at' this session. It is well
known that finance is the most sensitive fiber in the
social organism. It is that which is most easily dis
arranged, and when disordered produces the most
widespread disturbances. Any change thatjs to be
made in the currency of a country ought to be made
in times of prosperity, when industry is on a firm
basis, commerce flourishing and every condition of
the people so sound and secure that there will be
no danger of a panic or other disaster during the
period that the change is being carried out.
WHEN the present session of Congress be
gan there was Â¦'every prospect, that a com
prehensive bill dealing with the currency
problems of the country would' be promptly en
acted.: Later on Chairman Fowler of the House
Committee on Banking and Currency announced
that he was sanguine the House would give imme
diate consideration to a measure to that effect re
ported favorably from his committee. Of late, how
ever, the outlook has been by no means so promis
ing. Bad management on the part of some of the
leaders of the majority has complicated the situation.
At the present time the isthmian canal bill, the mer
chant marine, bill and the currency bill are seemingly
sidetracked, and unless earnest efforts be made to
procure action upon them they may be passed over
to the next session. .

The Theosophical Society also honored
the memory of its founder, Blavatsky at
the Pacific Coast headquarters. Odd Fel
lows' Hall, last night. When Mme. Bla
vatsky died. May 8, 1891. she requested
that memorial services be held on that
date and portions of the books "Light of
Asia and "Bhagavad Gita" were read
Among those who spoke were: Mrs. K.
B. Davis, Thomas Prime and Dr. Jerome
A. Anderson.
San Francisco Lodge of the Universal
Brotherhood Theosophlcal Society held a
public meeting at 310 O'Farrell street last
evening. The event was the celebration
of "White Lotus Day," in commemoration
of the life and work of H. P. Blavatsky,
who founded the Theosophical Society in
New York in 1875. Music and readings
from the sacred scriptures formed part of
the programme. Dr. Allen Griffiths deliv
ered an address on the character and
work of the theosophical leaders of the
nineteenth century. In part he said:
"The whole world owes Blavatsky a
debt of gratitude as one of Its redeemers.
While the few may be tardy in acknowl
edgment of her as one of humanity's
helpers the masses have already begun to
recognize her as such. The souls of men
are awakening to the Inspiring conscious
ness that they are divine; that the only
thing to fear is wrongdoing; that what
men call 'evil" Is the result of their own
Ignorance and selfishness in the present
or in past lives, and that each man as
a divine soul is the supreme arbitrator of
his own destiny and has the opportunity
to make that destiny godlike by thought
and deeds of unselfish service for his
brother souls."

Name of Their Society's
Founder.

The directors of the United Railroads
met yesterday in the Wells-Fargo build
ing to appoint a successor to E. P. Vln
ing. Many of the local men were dis
cussed, including H. H. Lynch and Su

visable to remove any of -them. It was

Vininir will be brought from the East.
"Will Examine Pupils' Teeth.
Frank L. Platt, chairman of a Joint
committee of the State Dental Associa
tion and the San Francisco Dental Asso
ciation has asked the co-operation of the
Board of Education in a proposed move
ment to examine the teeth of the pupils
in public schools for the purpose of se
curing data regarding the prevalance of
dental diseases. It Is Intended to base
thereon a course of instruction as to the
care and preservation of the teeth.

INVESTS WITHSCAPUIiAR.
Throughout the day people from all di
rections came to St. Francis, for the cere
mony extended far into the evening.
Lastly, shun bad company; avoid the com
panionship of corrupt boys and girls. One
corrupt and vicious boy or girl is sufficient to
ruin hundreds. Just as one drop of ink let
fall into a glass of clear, pure water will black
en the whole glassful, so one vicious youth
can blacken and taint the virtue of hundreds
cf boys and girls.
Secondly, avoid the reading of dangerous
publications which appear in the form of dime
novels and certain pictorial newspapers. As
ycu would destroy the eggs of serpents because
they bring forth young serpents that would
sting you to death, so you should destroy bad
books because they beget sins that kill the
soul. Bad publications are the most effectual
means In the hands of Satan for the ruin of
souls.
On this beautiful May morning of your first
communion you should resolve first, to receive
the holy eucharist frequently, for "unless you
eai of the flesh of the Son of man and drink
of his blood you shall not have life In you."
PASTOR ADVISES YOUTH.
My D*>ar Children: You have Just now re
ceived your first holy communion, or In other
words you have received the holy eucharlst.
which contains the body and blood, soul and
divinity of our Lord Jesus Christ under the
appearance of bread and wine. Therefore your
Joy and happiness should be exceedingly great
today. Did a King or a Queen or the Presi
dent of the United States pay you a visit In
your homes you would feel highly honored,,
bur how highly honored should you feel this
morning, for . your souls have been visited by
your Redeemer, who is the King of kings, the
Lord of lords and the Creator of the heavens
and the earth.

KILL THE DANDBUET GEBM
Or Your Hair "Will Fall Out Till You
Modern science has discovered that
dandruff Is caused by a germ that digs up
the scalp in scales as It burrows down to
the roots of the hair, -where it destroys
the hair's vitality, causing falling hair
and ultimately baldness. After Professor
Unna of Hamburg, Germany, discovered
the dandruff germ all efforts to find a
remedy failed until the great laboratory
discovery was made which resulted In
Newbro's Herpicide. It alone of all other
hair preparations kills the dandruff germ.
Without dandruff, hair grows luxuriantly.
"Destroy the cause, you remove the ef-

NATIONAL LEAGUE.
It is the prevailing opinion here that
unless the operators agree to arbitration
or make some . counter proposition which
will conta'in a substantial concession. the
committee of miners will declare for a
strike. ' - -Â¦;.. -'
To-night a telegram was received
through a private source stating that Sen
ator Hanna had declared unequivocally
that the report that arbitration had been
refused by the operators was not true. ,
Telegrams announcing this action were
sent to the presidents of the nine big coal
collieries and several officers of the Civic
Federation. President Mitchell declined
to make public the full text of the tele
grams. â¢ ' " . Â¦.â¢-..-...- â¢
The executive committee of the anthracite
mine workers' organization have proposed that
all questions at issue between themselves and
the anthracite mine operators be submitted to
an impartial board of arbitration, such board
to be selected by the industrial . branch of the
National Civic Federation. This proposition has
been sent to the operators by wire. .The com
mittees are now awaiting replies. .
This proposal was evidently agreed upon
informally during the night, for it was
only forty-five minutes from the time the
meeting convened until .the adjournment.
President Mitchell at the close of the ses
sion gave out the following statement:
SCR ANTON/ Pa., May: 8.-To-night the
situation in the threatened miners' strike
hangs pn answers that are expected to
come , to a proposal â¢ of Â¦ arbitration em
anating from this morning's session of
the miners', convention. . : -
ALBANY, N. Y., May 8.â Judge Earl,
referee in the matter of the constitution
ality of the New York State franchise tax
law, declarers the . law â¢ constitutional on
every point. The case is still to be- passed
upon by the appellate division of the.Su
preme Court and the Court of Appeals.
The special, franchise tax law ,was
passed in 18S9. In effect it declares that
corporation franchises are/ property and
must be taxed as such. Under the law
valuations of speclafVtranchlses are fixed
by the State Tax Commissioners. J
The amount of the assessment- involved
in these proceedings is $137,000,000. Forty
millions of this is property which under
the old regime was taxed by/ the local as
sessors. The total propertyiof this char
acter throughout the State is $266,000,000.
The amount of real estate as distin
guished from the naked franchises con
tained In this amount exceeds $100,000.
Judge Brarl â¢ takes up four' propositions
in his review of the law: 'First, conflict
with the State constitution on home rule;
second, confliclT with the Federal consti
tution; third, on impairing/ contracts and
Incompatibility of the law 'with , other tax
statutes,. in that the value of a franchise
for use of a street cannot be separated
from other property values; fourth, "gen
eral objections to the method of determin
ing values. On all^of these pfelnts he dis
sents from the objections and finds for
the State. - - .1 Â¦ Â¦

SALT LAKE, Utah. May 8.â A move
ment has been started among the sport
lovers of this city to organize a club and
offer a purse to Jim Jeffries and Bob
Fitzsimmons to meet here in ia twenty
round contest during the Elks* National
Convention in August. It is the intention
to offer the heavy-weights a purse of $20,
000. If successful in getting the signatures
of the men for a contest here it is planned
to have the fight take place in the oval
of the Salt Palace saucer track, the ring
to be located out in the center in the
open. The seating capacity of the amphi
theater can easily be made twenty thou
sand, and with the vast crowds . that will
be here from all over the country' to at
tend the Elks* meeting no question of the
financial success of the undertaking is
entertained.

LAW IS UPHELD
THE Columbia Theater has never
held a more select or apprecia
tive audience than that of yes
terday afternoon which applaud
ed the efforts of those who will
ingly gave their services to aid the Act
ors' Home, which was dedicated in New
York yesterday. By their attendance
theater-goers added $2000 to the fund that
has been raised .to advance this worthy
charity.
Four solid hours of comedy, singing,
comic opera and^ vaudeville was served
up for the entertainment of the audience.
Interspersed was a speech by. Milton No
bles, which was a classic in itself. The
entertainment went through -without a
hitch, owine to the able work -of L. E.
Woodthorpe and his assistants.
Promptly at 1:30 o'clock the curtain rose
on the third act of "The Two Escutch
eons," splendidly played by the Alcazar
Stock Company. The members of the
organization threw themselves Into their
lines and never were their efforts more
appreciated. It was a fitting introduc
tion for what followed. Miss Georgie
MINERS STRIKE
MAY BE AVERTED
The committee having the affair in
hand worked energetically and Its efforts
were crowned with success. Milton No
bles and Ralph Pincus did excellent works
Selby Oppenheimer gathered the volun
teers together and sent them on their way
rejoicing in rubber-tired carriages; the
men behind the scenes worked like beav
ers setting the stage for acts of every
kind, and the musicians played with un
usual interest in their work. 1 As a re
sult of their services a large sum will
be sent to Al Hayman, president of the
Actors' "Home Fund. . â¢
appreciated. At the end of the act they
were accorded five curtain calls. Arthur
Cunningham, of the Tivoli, next sang.
He rendered a number of good songs.
Edward Harrigan and company came
from the Grand Opera-house to play a
scene from "Old Lavender." Mr. Harri
pan rendered two songs that were well
received. The Florpdora sextet of chil
dren, from the Central Theater, was put
on too late in the programme, but the
little ones scored their usual hit. The
Hoste eccentric acrobats, f 1 om
the Orpheum, completed one of the best
benefit programmes ever : given in this
city.
Los Angeles is well represented in San
Francisco at this time if there are to be
any negotiations to-morrow. Manager
Morley of the ball club and Al Levy and
L. J. Christopher, all of whom are in the
Century Athletic Club, are in the northern
city.
Among the local talent who follow the
movements of the men in the squared
circle, the latest ultimatum of Fitzsim
mons is something of a puzzle and is in
terpreted to mean that he may. be looking
for a hole to crawl through. About the mid
dle of 'April Fitzsimmons sent a telegram
offering to meet Jeffries at-any lime or
place in California that Jeffrie^might
name. He no sooner gets in this State
than he becomes dictatorial again, for
getting that it is not his right to have all
the say In the matter. There is a general
impression that Jim Kennedy may have
been advising Fitzsimmons since the Cor-'
nishman's arrival in San Francisco . and
have put the idea, into Fitzsimmons' head
that there is nothing to be looked for in
this city. ,
There is an added proviso to this state
ment that the 'proposition of the San
Francisco club which handles the contest
shall be such as the participants shall se
cure as much net as the Los Angeles
proposition will give them. The Los An
geles proposal is a flat offer of $25,000 guar
anteed, with an alternative of 80 per cent
of the gross receipts.

OAKLAND.
Hannivan accepted an almost impossi
ble chance of Walters' foul drive in the
first. The ball was about to drop in short
left field when Hannivan got there with
one mitt and made one of the prettiest
catches seen on the local field this year.
Mohler played his u^ual brilliant game at
second, while Toman was there with his
usual errors. Mohler stole second wiiile
McPartlln held the ball in the first. It
was a daring play and the little fellow
made a great slide past Raymer, who
had the ball and was waiting to tag him.
The score:
The feature of the game was House
holder's" batting. Out of five .times up he
secured three two-cushion swipes and a
single. Raj'mer got three -hits and - four
runs. Old Pete Lohman was again in
great form, and only one Angel stole sec
ond on him.
It all happened in the fifth. Oakland
was getting into the game and doing
things to Slowball McPartlin. They
chased three over the route when the con
flagration occurred. Some were unkind
enough to lay the blame on Morley be
cause he thought Ewing would win out,
while others thought Waddell did the
<3amge so that he could show the multi
tude how he used to fight fires in Mis
souri. â¢ , , ,
At all events the Rube made good in
doing fireman's stunts. He had Fitzpat
rick beat a block. He carried the water
buekets with both hands and then sig
naled to the man behind the bar to bring
forth the beer, as the water supply was
running low. The bartender refused to
comply with Rube's request, so the rest
of the bunch compromised by putting on
the hose, and the only lively incident of
the contest was spoiled.
The Angels mistook Hodson for a ping
pong server, and swatted his offerings to
the four ends of the lot. They hit safely
three times in the initial trial, and Toman
ecored. With three on the sacks in the
third. Householder drove a two-bagger to
left, and they all came in. A couple more
safe taps brought in two more, and made
the scorfe 6 to L
Morley' s men completed their run-get
ting in the fifth and sixth by bunching a
few more hits at opportune times. They
seemed to land on Hodson at will, and al
ways put the ball -vtaere the fielders could
not put their mitts near It.
The fire spoiled Oakland's chances.
They had just begun to discover what
Slowball's delivery looked like, and were
making him look like an empty beer
schooner, when the fire happened and put
the kibosh on the good work. However,
they succeeded in landing one in the first
and three in the fifth.
The last two tallies happened in Oak
land's half of the sixth, and in a rather
peculiar manner. Schmidt doubled to left
field and Gorton followed with one that
resembled Schmidt's, even to the bound
it took. Gorton soon after came in on
Walters' hit to right, which put an end to
the making of runs.
It was Morley day at Recreation Park
yesterday. His men won the game and
put out the fire. The Angels landed on
Hodson for only fifteen hits in six in
nings, and to make the game interesting
some enterprising gent set fire to the pad
ded backstop. An excited individual yelled
"Fire! 5 ' and the official scorer ran from
the grounds and tore up Eighth street.
He was brought back in a cab and play
was resumed.
Fitzsimmons was on the streets yester
day renewing old acquaintances. He has
a fresh, youthful appearance and is full
of boyish spirits. He claims he is a bet
ter fighter to-day than he was twenty
years ago. He knows the game better in
all its departments, having made a care
ful study of boxing in recent years. Te
praises the modern system of training,
which builds up a man's vitality. In for
mer times ponderous muscles were devel
oped, but at the expense of the boxer's
vitality, that intangible quality which can
neither be seen or measured, but which
plays so important a part in all contests
In which men or horses take part
It needs only a few minutes' conversa
tion with Fitzsimmons to see he is in
earnest about the matter. He claims that
had Jeffries accepted the Charleston Ex
position management's offer quickly there
would have been no opposition. Accord
ing to Fitzsimmons, the Governor of
South Carolina had told the exposition
people to go ahead and arrange the
match. When there was so much talk
about it the Governor could not withstand
the pressure and gave out the statement
that he would not permit the fight.
The opinion was freely expressed among
sporting men yesterday that, the manly
thing for Jeffries to do would be to board
the first train for this city and talk over
the match. When the men. were separated
by the width of the continent they carried
on a long-distance talking match of
which the public grew tired. If Jeffries
determines to stay in the south the pros
pects are good for a repetition of the con
troversy.
Matters seem to be ,at a serio-comic
deadlock, as Fitzsimmons refuses to con
duct any negotiations through Billy De
laney, Jeffries' manager, blaming the lat
ter for placing him- in the position before
the public of having avoided the oppor
tunity of making a match. Fitzsimmons
will not make the pilgrimage to Los An
geles, believing he has shown his earnest
ness in the matter by coming across the
continent. -
No progress was made yesterday in the
negotiations for a ftght between Jeffries
and Fitzsimmons.
Former Will Not Treat With
Billy Delaney Nor Go
to Los Angeles.

Anarchy and Anarchists the
Subject of Several Inter
esting Papers.
LOUISVILLE, Ky., May 8.â At to-day's
session of the Association of Police Chiefs
of the United States and Canada, Presi
dent Sylvester read letters from numer
ous prominent police officials in foreign
countries and the English, Chinese and
Spanish Ministers at Washington, indors
ing the work of the association. All the
European police officials are heartily in
favor of an international bureau of-iden
tification. On motion of Chief Dietach of
Cincinnati, all the foreigners were ten
dered a vote of thanks.
At the meeting of the board of directors
of the Bureau of Identification, Colonel
Phil Deitsch of Cincinnati was re-elected
president and E. A. Evans of Chicago su
perintendent. Colonel Deitsch read his
annual report, showing the good results
secured by the use of the Bertlllon sys
tem. It was decided that the Bertlllon
clerks of the various- police departments
should meet in Washington, D. C, to
agree upon a uniform method of measur
ing criminals. Another meeting of the
board will be held to-night, when papers
will be read on professional criminals.
Chief Kiely of St. Louis read a paper on
"Can Criminals Reform?" holding that
they could be reformed.
Chief of Police Francis O'Neill of Chi
cago read a paper on "Anarchy and An
archists." He said the American an
archists were much like the Russian ni
hilists. He said there were a great num
ber of anarchists in Chicago, but they
were no longer defiant and they were abla
to make no moves which, were not known

Chief William Price of Vlcksburg. MI33.,

criminals could not be reformed.

by Roderick Ross. Chief of- the Edin
burgh (Scotland) Constabulary, was read
by Secretary Carr. Ross believed in the
confinement of anarchists until they were
willing to abide by the ' law. and said
that those* who incited others to crimo
should be confined for life.
Chief of police Henry Muth of Alle
gheny, speaking on "Crime," said that
the opium habit is responsible for mor*
crime than any other vice.
Afte? listening to the reading of several

til to-morrow.
Ten years ago Sunday, William Greer
Harrison, then, as now, president of the
Olympic Athletic Club, walked out into
Market street and Grant avenue and,
marking off a line on the stone-paved
street with his walkingstlck, said: "We
will start here."
This was for the first bicycle relay race
ever held around the bay of San Fran
cisco, a distance of 100 miles. Each re
curring year this remarkable race has
been run, and from the first it has not
lost one iota of its popularity. . At no
place in the world has such an ideal
course been discovered as that over which
the race is run.
There are fiye evenly balanced teams
entered for the race on Sunday, represent
ing Bay k City Wheelmen, California Cy
cling Club, Garden City "Wheelmen, Oak
land Wheelmen and San Francisco Wheel
men. On paper the. Bay City Wheelmen
and Garden City Wheelmen have strong
teams, but the slightest accident or delay
may upset the rosiest chances. The
couriers in the order in which they will
ride on Sunday are as follows:

IN NEED OF ASSISTANCE

Fremantle.
MELBOURNE. Victoria, May 8.â A life
boat containing the first officer and three
men of the British ship Boveric has ar
rived at Fremantle, Western Australia,
after having sailed 1500 miles, to report
that the Boveric lost her propeller when
1500 miles from Fremantle and was in
need of assistance.
The Boveric sailed from this port for
Natal March 21 with remounts and ninety
soldiers on board.

MOLDEES ABE SUSTAINED
BY NATIONAL COMMITTEE
Shutting Down or the Union Pacific
Foundry Is Declared to Be a
Lockout.
OMAHA, May 8.â A telegram" was re
ceived to-day by the local head of the
Molders' Union statingr that the shutting
down of the Union Pacific Foundry here
has been declared a lockout by the execu
tive board of the national body and that
union molders all over the country will
be instructed to not work on castings
for that company. The Union Pacific
Foundry is having its castings made at
the Featherstone Foundry in Chicago and
that company is expected to be the first
affected by the order.
Molders here say the machinists and
carmen's unions in the local shops will be
called on to assist them if castings- made
by non-union men are sent to this city

LOS ANGELES, May 8.â The remains

tional Cemetery at Arlington will be at
tended with all military honor.-

SANTA BARBARA. May 8.â Miss Net
tie Harding, a prominent young lady of
this city, was the victim of a mysterious
and brutal assault last night. She had
gone to a neighbor's and was returning
home when she was struck on the head
by a man carrying a heavy slungshot. She
was rendered unconscious by the first
blow, but the brute continued his attack,
striking her about the shoulders and
limbs. He made no attempt at robbery
and seemed bent only on the death of hi3
victim. Miss Harding is resting easy to
day, and her Â¦wounds, while serious, will
not prove fatal. There is no clew to her
assailant.
Ruffian Attacks a Young Woman.

The bank records for April, 1900, were
to-day produced in court for the purpose
of showing that four checks certified to
by the Crocker- Woolworth National Bank
of San Francisco had been given. Upon
the careful examination of these records
Attorney Meldon for the defendant was
decidedly taken back when he could find
no such entries as he insinuated at the
commencement of the case would be
there. William Ebner, paying teller for
the bank, was put on the stand to testify
as to the modus operandi of keeping
track of certified checks, especially along
about April 2ti, 1900, but hi3 answers were
evidently not what was expected by thd
defendant's counsel, and further Question
ing of the witness was dispensed with.
Attorney Meldon made a motion tor
nonsuit on the gTound of insufficiency of
evidence, but it was denied. ! An adjourn
ment was taken until 9 o'clock to-mor
row, at which time Charles Fair and Her
man Oelrichs are expected to be put on
hte stand.

Superintendent Hughes of Alameda then
addressed the convention on the advant
ages and disadvantages of departmental
work. He placed emphasis on the fact
that the teacher must at all times work
with the pupil, thus leading him on to
independence. /The material must be
given to the pupil in Its most interesting
form and the teacher must be an enthu
siast. Hence, specialization, which has
been long recognized as being necessary
in universities and colleges, will soon be
required in high and grammar schools.
The constant changing of teachers i3
wrong and leaves a scattered impression
on the pupil's mind at the end of the
course

SAN JOSE, May 8.â The canker worm
Is appearing in great numbers in the
orchards of the valley, and fruit-growefs
are now waging vigorous war on them.
The first reports of the pest came in last
week. Reports to-day say they have in
creased greatly in numbers during ths
past three or four, days of warm weather.
The cherry trees are the greatest suffer
ers. - Some orchards that a week ago
promised ;big returns have been ruined.
Hundreds of men are engaged In fighting
the pests. 'Â¦:-.- ,- : . ...

The breaking of a water hydrant In front
of the North Point dock grain warehouses at
the corner of Lombard and Sansome streets,
caused a lot of damage yesterday morning and
tho runaway horses responsible for the smash
up were, saved from drowning only by being
cut free from a wrecked sand wagon,, the
wheels of which fouled a knocked-down lamp
post and the remains of the hydrant. The
horees, belonging to Jerry Sullivan, got be
yond the control of Louis Jones, the driver.
The frightened animals ran down Sansome
street and when opposite the grain ware
houses the wheels of the heavy wagon locked
around a lamppost, snapping it off like a pipe
stem. As the horses struggled for freedom
they managed to knock off the top of a water
hydrant. -A torrent of water shot fifty feet
into the air. The horses were freed from tho
wreck with the aid of sharp knives, but the
wa{er played for nearly an hour. Part of the
Sansome street car track was inundated and
the grain warehouses were flooded> The dam
age by water is said to amount to more j than
$1000.
Horses Wreck Hydrant.

By virtue cf a dispensation granted by
the illustrious imperial potentate, Philip
C. Shaffer, a . special session of Islam
Temple, Mystic Shrine of San Francisco,
will be held in' the city of Reno, Nev.,
to-morrow evening', at 7:30 o'clock for
the reception of petitions and f<5r 'cere
monies. The. members .will leave to
night at 6 o'clock from the foot of Market
street and arrive at Reno at 8:10 o'clock
to-morrow morning. There will be a
special train. of Pullman coaches. \ r More
than one hundred nobles of Islam Tem
ple have-already engaged berths In this
train, and it ia expected that there will
be a great many more. There will be a
fcido trip, which has been arranged by the
nobles .of Reno, to Virginia' City, where
the party will be entertained and piloted
through some of the celebrated mines of
the Comstock lode. The members will
also visit Carson City. It is expected that
this will be one of the most memorable
trips of Islam Temple. The party will
return here Sunday afternoon.

The United States Circuit Court of Ap
peals yesterday listened to the arguments
in the case of the Northern Pacific Rail
way Company vs. P. H. Tynan, admin
istrator of the estate of John Tynan, de?
ceased. . John Tynan was a brakeman and
was crushed to death between two cars
while coupling them. His estate was
awarded $10,000 damages in the District
Court of Oregon, and the railroad com
pany has appealed. B. S. Grosscup made
the argument for the appellant and Dan
J. Malarkey spoke for the Tynan estate.
The case was thereupon submitted.
