The inspectors found that E. Andersen,
chief mate of the Noyo, was negligent in
leaving the bridge in charge of an un
licensed man, who did not know the sig
nals to the engine room, when the steam
er was running at full speed and the
weathei was foggy. His license was re
voked. The license of Peter Hedwell, sec
ond mate of the San Pedro, was suspend
ed for thirty days for negligence In run
ning the San Pedro at full speed with a
squan sail set In foggy weather on his
own responsibility and not i calling the
masttr, as he should have done. After he
eaw the Noyo he did all he could to avoid
the collision. The masters, H. Johnsen of
the Ntvo and E. Jahnsen of the San
Pedro, were exonerated.
Captain O. F. Bodies and John K. Bul
ger punished two mates yesterday whom
they found guilty of carelessness -and
negligence In navigating the steamers
Noyo and San Pedro. The steamers col
lided at 1 o'clock in the morning on
April 18, thirty miles northwest of Point
Reyes. The Noyo was damaged to the
extent of $4000 <and the San -Pedro 15000.
The lumber cargo was damaged to the ez
tent or ?2000.

.. Vigorous efforts are being made. by the
educational committee of the Young Men's
Christian Association to, render the class
work carried on in the evening more ef
fective in supplying the means of self
help to the young men of San Francisco.
With this end in view they have estab
lished continuous work throughout the
year in'commercial branches, steam en
gineering and naval architecture. A larg?
number of the five hundred students are
continuing work during the summer, and
.many students have entered = the classes
for tho first time. Plans for a greatly
improved equipment and enlarged accom
modations are being pushed with vigor,
but the indications are that -the capacity
of * the rooms available for educational
purposes will be severely taxed this year.

Mrs. Paulsen alleges that her husband
left her on February 16 of last year and
that since that time she has received no
monetary or other aid from him. She al
leges further that their child is , sickly
and delicate, and that because of her
husband's neglect she has been unable to
give him the care and attention his con
dition requires.
She also alleges that Paulsen is fully
aware of his child's condition and need
for medical treatment, and that he has
neglected and refused to give her any
assistance. She charges that Paulsen is
in possession of considerable property, ac
quired after his marriage, and therefore
well able to care for her and their child.
He also receives, she alleges, a salary of
$200 a month from Siebe Bros. & Plage
mann.'
The Paulsens were married five years
ago and reside at 1722 Eddy street. * They
have one child, a boy, aged 4 years.

I Mra. Pereira had made an'appointment to be
hero, this afternoon at 2:30 o'clock to have
8i,mÂ»i: teeth extracted. He% daughter had been
a patient of mine, but this was the first time
I had treated her^mother. When Mrs. Pereira
came she told me that she had suffered Intense
ly from toothache last night, and she desired
that the aching -teeth should be extracted.
\ After she had taken her place in the operat
ing, chair I made ordinary preparations for the
operation. There was no gas or ether, admin
istered, . only a local application of cocaine..
It was Just after I had removed the fourth
tooth that she suddenly cried. "Oh, my heart!"
and collapsed. At first I thought she had only
fainted, as patients sometimes do under ex
tracting operations. I got some whisky and
administered it, aflso trying artificial respira
tion, but she showed no signs of reviving.
Then I called Dr. Goldson. a, woman physician
next door, and Dr. Hornef and Dr. Hyde, who
were In my other offices, also came in. Later
Dr. L.arkey was summoned.. Every effort to
restore the" lady was made, but the doctors
wore finally compelled to declare that she was
dead. ; .
SUBJECT TO HEART TROUBLE.
,_ Under the circumstances I could do nothing
else but notify- the Coroner, which I did
promptly. ;My distress over this unfortunate
affair is very great, but I feel that it was such
an occurrence that could not be foreseen or in
any manner forestalled. After her v death, I
learned that Mrs. Pereira had been subject Â¦ to
spasms, evidently due to weakened action of
the heart. ' . :
Her death occurred about 0:30 o'clock, be
cause she had been at the office an hour when
she collapsed.
"Miss Pereira told me that her mother
had been subject to frequent attacks of
heart trouble," said Dr. S. C. Hornef.
"The indications are that heart disease
was the cause of her sudden collapse and
death."
The .Pereiras have resided for several
years in this city. The family is prom
inent among the and South
American colonies' in San Francisco.
Madame Pereira was a native of Chile
and 41 years of age. The funeral will be
held Saturday afternoon at 2 o'clock from
the family residence. The body will prob
ably be placed in a receiving vault and
later sent to Chile.
Coroner Mehrmann will hold an Inquest
Saturday evening.
Dr. Schmidt was greatly distressed over
the untefward demise of hi3 patient. He
said the sudden collapse was the first sug
gestion of anything wrong.
Consul Pereira Â¦was overcome with grief
and with his motherless daughter retired
to their residence, 961 Madison street,
stunned by the sudden blow.
A post mortem examination will be
necessary to definitely determine the
cause of death. Miss Pereira told the
dentist's associates that her "mother had
been subject to attacks of heart trouble.

The Oakland Federated Trades Council,
which is the central body recognized by
-the carpenters, is going to try and orga
nize local plasterers' and plumbers' un
ions in opposition to those unions that
have stood by the Building Trades Coun
cil, and the Building Trades Council has
a plan for an opposition carpenters' union.
But ihe Building Trades Council ha3
taken the bit In Its teeth and has adopted
resolutions declaring that no members of
unions affiliated with the Alameda Coun
ty Building Trades Council will be per
mitted to work with those who do not
hold the regular quarterly working cardd
of the State Building Trades Council. The
date for I the enforcement of this order
has been set for May 17. As members of
the Carpenters' Union do not hold these
cards this means that the members of the
Building Trades Council will not longer
recognize them as union men.
The war that is on between, the San
Francisco Building Trades Council and
the; Carpenters' Union has extended to
this city. The Building Trades Council
and the carpenters have been at out3 for
some time, the carpenters having with
drawn from the Building, Trades Council
and recognized the American Federation
of Labor, but up to the present time each
side has recognized the other as being
union, and men representing the two sides
have worked side by side upon the same
buildings.

1118 Broadway, May 8.

THE NEW TORPEDO-BOAT, DE
STROYER WHICH HAS PASS
ED A FINAL. TRIAL.
Good, luck attended the launching of
the handsome steel tug Sea Rover last
night. at the Fulton Engineering and
Shipbuilding Yards. Harbor View.
Punctually at 11 o'clock little Hazel
Hanell, daughter of Harry Hanell, chief
engineer of the works, ascended the plat
form and with the breaking of a bottle
of champagne over the/ bows she spoKe
the words. "I christen thee Sea Rover."
The vessel gracefully glided , through an
avenue of electric lights and several hun
dred cheering people into the waters of
the bay. Â¦ . ,
Whistles of steamships sounded their
shrieking congratulations, and voices of
the witnesses of the interesting ceremony
sent up ringing cheers, and the flag of
the United States fluttered proudly at the
.Dow and stern
TRIUMPH FOR BUILDERS.
It was a triumph for the shipbuilders.'
the designer and the owners, the Ship
owners' and Merchants' Tugboat Com
pany,"and also an evidence of the triumph
of the shipbuilding industry of San Fran
cisco. '.
A- prettier or "more unusual sight could
not be imagined than the launching o'
the "Sea Rover" as she glided away from
the illumination of the scores tÂ»f powerful :
electric lights; Into the dark of the night.
The tug awaiting to take her in tow j
looked like a spectre as she came stealing
quietly alongside of her, and the people
who dotted the shore waving their white
handkerchiefs and hats in the air seemed
like so many 'phantoms.
The launching had , been wisely post
poned by James Spiers, Jr., the general,
manager, from the previous night that
the tide might possibly, assure more safety
for "the operation.
His judgment proved to be good, for the
tide last night: was all that could be de
sired and consequently there were hearty
congratulations exchanged between all
concerned.
DIMENSIONS OF VESSEL.
The "Sea Rover" is a steel boat de
signed by A. Austin Sperry and is of the
following dimensions:
Length over all, 128 feet; length between
perpendicular, 120 feet; beam molded, 24 feet
Â« Inches; depth at side, 15 feet. Her hull is
steel and engines compound, cylinders 17 Inches
and 41 inches, stroke 28 inches; boiler is 10
feet 6 inches In diameter and 16 feet 10 inches
Ion*: steam steering gear; luel capacity, 50,000
gallons of oil.
', Among .those who witnessed the night
launching of the "Sea Rover" were:
Mrs. D. H. Dexter. Mrs. Jennie Laws. Mrs.
Louis Kwlatkowskl. Mr. and Mrs. â¢ W. A. Fol
ger, Mre. G. " W. DIckson. Miss. Lucy White.

liam F. Hanell Miss May Haley, Miss Ceclle
O'Brien.. Miss May Law, Miss Zoe Elgle, Karl
J Peterson. James Spiers Jr., A. Austin Sper
ry Harry Hanell. and many of the employes
of the Fulton Engineering and Shipbuilding
Works.
Many* of Mr. 1 Sperry's and Mr. Spier's
friends were aboard the "Sea Rover"
when she was launched and were after
ward transferred to the tug and landed
at one of the city's wharves.

The Perry will be the fastest destroyer
in the navy. She did better than thirty
knots on her last trial, but the ferry-boat
captains that saw her shoot down the bay
yesterday are all ready to swear that she
was traveling at more than sixty miles
an hour.
Henry T. Scott said yesterday that he
had received no details of the destroyer's
performances, but had been informed that
she had given satisfaction in every par
ticular and had been accepted by the
Government.
The Perry as first turned out was a
failure. Her model proved unsatisfactory.
She was speedy, but when she traveled
fast she dragged too much of the bay
after her. Her stern was remodeled and
now Ehe reels off thirty knots without
disturbing the surrounding water to any
appreciable extent.
Captain Fenton of the Farragut has had
charge of the recent trials, and Captain
George Harvey has acted as pilot. The
Perry will be sent to Mare Island and be
placed in commission just as soon as the
formal transfer has been made.
tract requirements and will be
turned over to the Government in a few
days. The last trial was made yesterday,
when she gave a continuous performance
which satisfied the naval board in charge
that the Union Iron Works had delivered
the goods and that the Perry was worthy
of adoption by Uncle Sam.

frequent to amount to tribula
tions the torpedo-boat destroyer
Perry has fulfilled all her con-
There Is absolutely no difficulty with the
brotherhood, and when the few who are stir
ring up this contention cease their reprehensi
ble conduct the matter will be settled and
Tade out of existence. This is the sentiment
>f thirty-seven out of thirty-nine unions of the
ouilding trades, and when the disgruntled two
art- refused support by the outside mischief
aiakers the matter will be at an end.
Seventh, that since the controversy began
:he membership of 4s:{ has dwindled down from
WO members <clalmed) to 1R+ <as shown by the
rote on May 5. J0C2). and since the last named
late others have withdrawn, leaving little or
no opposition to the Hulldlng Trades Council.
Taking these facts into consideration, the
Building Trades Council proposes to continue in
the same manner that it has thus far success
fully managed the mechanical end of the build
ing business in this city, and does not propose
to have outside influences magnify an insig
nificant controversy in which 150 disgruntled
fermonm ere endeavoring- to create strife and
trouble in the building business. The con
iractors understand the situation and are sat
.fified; the journeymen are satisfied and are
working.
Sixth, that the Master Builders of San Fran
cisco, having investigated the question, have
heard both sides and have decided, unani
mously, to sustain the Building Trades Coun
cil.
Fifth, that every member of every carpen
ter's union has three timiÂ»s voted upon the
fubject, first as above stated, second by a vote
nf seven to one in favor of the Building: Traded
Council, and third, by a vote of 381 to 18 sus
la'ninir the Building Trades Council.
Fourth, that every member of every union
affiliated with the Building Trades Council
that voted on the constitution and by a vote
of five in favor to one against, has sustained
the position of the Building Trades Council.
Third, that paid action Is prosecuted by H.
W. Hutton. appointed by llayor Schmitz as
Police Commissioner, and counsel for the
unions affiliated with the San Francisco Labor
Council and the Musicians' Mutual Protective
Association, of which Mayor Schmitz is presi
dent. Is a member.
Second, thai Union 4S3 has taken the mai
ls.- into court and has sought by injunction to
goiern the affairs ot organized labor.
First, that the Building Trades Council is
fu!ly capable of taking tare of its own busi
ness.
The public prints of to-day contained state
ments to the effect that Mayor Schmitz pro
poses to adjust the difficulty now existing be
iv.epii this Council and 483.
Wo strenuously resent this action or pro
pcfi-d action ujwn the following grounds:
The Mayor's efforts to adjust the dif
ferences of the warring factions may not
result in much, however. In view of the
following statement, which was issued
last evening by the Building Trades
Council:
KESENTS MAYOR'S ACTION.
Mayor Schmitz evidently fears that the
council may be brought into the contro
versy and he has accordingly asked to
see the officials of the council with a
view to arriving at a settlement of the
matter. An early consultation will be
held, and ail those who are interested in
the building trades are looking forward
to the future with anxiety. Meanwhile
tlie participants in the struggle are rest
ing on their oars, waiting to see what
will turn up.
It was currently reported last night that
Ihe Labor Council would take a hand in
the settlement of the trouble, inasmuch
as Carpenters' Union No. 4S3 is affiliated
with the council. It is claimed that if the
Building Trades Council becomes too ob
ttinate the Labor Council will simply
call out the stevedores and teamsters who
handle building materials. Shouid thia
action to taken the effect upon the build
ing industry at this time would be most
eerious. That the carpenters of Union
No. 483. which Is affiliated with the coun
cil, have the sympathy of the members
of the council goes without saying.
The business agents of the carpenters'
unions still affiliated with the Building
Trades Council called oft four men from
buildings yesterday because they Â¦were
working with members of Union No. 4S3.
which the council has tabooed. The men
were taken to other jobs and promptly
put to work. While the master builders
nave given their support to the Building
Trades Council, they have done nothing
thus far to precipitate an open rupturo
between the warring factions. The build
ing industry is exceedingly active at the
present time and carpenters are In good
demand. It follows therefore that when
a. carpenter who has a card from Union
No. 22 is called off from one job where
members of Union No. 4S3 are employed
he is taken at once to another job, where
he remains until his presence is discov
ered by the business agent, when the pro
cedure Is re-enacted. In this way the
building operations are not seriously af
fected, and it is now believed that the
trouble Is gradually dying: av.ay.
BUSINESS AGENTS ACTIVE.
The members of the brotherhood who
ere standing out against the Building
Trades* Council because of Its support ot
t'nion No. 22, which was suspended froiii
the brotherhood because of a violation of
the ruies of the brottifcrhood, are receiv
ing hearty support from loyal unions in
all parts of the State. At a meeting of
ibe new district council of the brotner
hood last night a series of resolutions
adopted by the union of carpenters at Point
Kichmond were received. The resolutions
indorsed the stand taken by the brother
hood locals and asserted that no member
of the Point Richmond Union would work,
with or recognize any member of Mc-
Carthy's Union No. Â£2. The members of
that union were designated as "scabs"
and all members of the brotherhood were
cautioned against working with any men
who were not affiliated with the brother
hood.
w
The troubles existing between the
Building Trades Council and the unions
of the United Brotherhood of Carpenters
and Joiners have not yet been settled. The
mediation of Mayor Schmitz has been In
vited and that official consulted witL sev
eral parties to the dispute yesterday. No
p!;in for the settlement of the controversy
was advanced, and it is hardly expected
that the matter will be adjusted within
the month. ';" . > /

NEW YORK, May 8. â Stocks recovered from
yesterday's weakness to-day, although there
was uncertainty manifest in the prevailing
eentiment. A more cheerful feeling over the
anthracite outlook is responsible for consider
able rebuying by short sellers of yesterday,
who professed confidence Â¦ last night that a
strike could not be averted. The hesitation
which developed In the market was due to a
considerable extent to the action taken by the
mine workers' convention, which presents an
entirely new phase of the situation. The ac
tive coaler* reflected no misgiving after the
announcement of the miners' officers to arbi
trate, but they fell into comparative dullness.
The high-priced coalers offered the anomaly
of early weakness, while the Readings were
advancing and a Btrong recovery after the de
mand for Readings had largely died out. The
whele market fell into dullness in the latter
part of the day while waiting for the announce
ment of the reply of the anthracite operators
to the offer of the miners. The result Is that
the day's transactions are not much over half
those of yesterday. Outside the Readings the
principal activity was in St. Paul, Union Pa
cific, the local tractions and Southern Railway,
t'nlon Pacific and SL Paul were bought neav-
Jly for Western account, the attraction being
attributed to crop conditions. The referee'*
decision upholding the constitutionality of the
New York franchise tax law Induced some ac
tive liquidation of the Ftocks of New Yorlc
public utilities', but they were well supported
and rallied. The advance in Louisville and
Scuthern Railway met realizing. Reports of
an apparent contest between the two Interests
lor control of coal lands increased the dispo
sition to cell, Illinois Central becoming strong
:n contrast to its weakness yesterday, while
the other two stocks were advancing. There
were evidences of etrength among minor stocks
which are supposed to be the subject of manip
ulation by speculative pools, although various
rumors contiued to circulate of their pros
pective absorption or merging. Tennessee Coal
end Colorado Fuel were under pressure. The
laÂ»t named, however, recovered to well above
i&Et night. International Power fell 9 points
lÂ»-low the laet Bale price, but fully recovered.
There was a tale of St. Lawrence and Adiron
dack at 52. compare! with the preceding, sale
et 138%. This sale was Iwfore the collapse of
the Webb-Meyer stocks. Tne Incidental state-

STOCK MARKET.
CHICAGO, May 8.â Bears who had been
hammering wheat prices on favorable weather
ccnoitions found too many bull factors arrayed
against them to-day. The weather was good
over nearly all the wheat belt and statistical
Journals issued bearishly favorable crop news,
but receipts were very small and the cash po
sition was very strong. Cables also were com
paratively firm, when depression : was taken
into account. After an early see-saw in prices
bulls took hold of the market and worked ad
vances. The cash situation has gradually de
veloped a strength that the traders eay prob
ably will Influence speculation for some time
Elevators have all the wheat in sight the
country is offering little and the elevator peo
ple have sold much of their holdings at July
prices rather than at the lower May prices
The result has been a scarcity of cash wheat
at the recently current May figure. To-day
May prices on outside markets advanced above
July and at one time here almost equaled them
Added to this was a good com strength. Good
cash sales were made late yesterday, fourteen
loads being taken for export. ' Shorts cov
ered freely and July, which opened U@Â« c to
V*@%c up at 74%c to 74%c, sold up to 74 %c
and closed firm, %@%c up. at 74%c. '**':
Corn ruled strongest of the grains to-day and
made .good advances. Traders generaly regard
the position of the bull party as such that any
opening by It the crowd quickly follows. July
closed firm, %Â«$%c up, at 61Vi@61%c. Â¦
Oats showed strength and activity. July
closed firm, %c up, at 30%c. y

ALEXANDER G. McADIE,
Forecast Official.
Northern California â Fair Friday, exceRt
showers In the foothills; continued warm
weather in the interior with light southerly
winds; brisk westerly winds on the coast, with
fog.
Southern California â Cloudy Friday; light
southwest winds.
Nevada â Cloudy Friday: continued warm;
brisk to high southwest winds.
In the treat valleys of California the temper
atures are slightly above normal. Very high
temperatures tare reported In the valley of the
Colorado and over Southern Arizona.
Forecast made at San Francisco for thirty
hours ending midnight. May 9, 1902:
No rain has fallen in California, but condi
tions are favorable for showers in the Sierras
and in the foothills.
There has been but little change !n pressure
during the past twenty-four hours over the
western half of the country. A moderate dis
turbance is working southward over the Rocky
Mountain region and thunderstorms with rain
are reported generally through Idaho and the
mountains of Utah.

WHEATâ The English markets were weak.

LONDON, May 8. â The offerings at the wool
auction sales numbered 15,710 bales. The
home trade and Continent were active buyers.
American representatives were reserved, occa
sionally purchasing a few good merinos anl
crossbreds at extreme rates. A good selection
of scoured was offered and sold freely to
France and Germany. Lamb's wool sold well
at an advance of 10 per cent.

Mail advices from London state that 'the
market for salmon is steady, with the con
sumptive demand improving. From Liverpool
It is reported that the good inquiry from the
country still continues, and there Is a large
and steady demand. Prices are well . main
tained and "there are indications of a slight ad
vance in one or two varieties.
SALMONâ This market is quiet, but the
Eastern markets are in good shape, and salmon
Is going into consumption more widely than
ever before. Fairly heavy consignments have
been made from New York to England. The
run on the Columbia River is fair for this pe
riod, and a large run is looked for later. The
Pacific Selling Company. has announced prices
at $1 50 for tails and $1 65 for flats. At these
rates, however, no business has as yet been
reported, except that which was done some
time ago, subject to opening prices. New York
jobbers "look upon these rates as too high. On
the Sacramento the run is not nearly so large
as last year at this time.

year.

"To-day's first hand stock consists of 8467
bags Costa Rica, 491 Nicaragua, 11,093 Salva
dor, 25,774 Guatemala. 2563 Mexican and 2374
bags of other kinds; in all 50,702 bags."
"Deliveries from' first bands from April 8 to
the present writing were nearly 18,000 bags,
considerably above the requirements for act
ual consumption during this period, but dem
onstrating a healthy market even if a lower
one, for meanwhile prices have sagged every
where. Receipts â¢ In Brazil govern the imme
diate situation and continue heavy, the world's
visible showing a further increase during April
of 135,000 bags. In this connection the mild
crops, now coming to market, must be counted
as factors, and for some weeks to come, but
indications are that receipts from those quar
ters will end more sharply than usual. New
York prices for Brazils are about %c per pound
lower during the past thirty days. The decline
here has fully corresponded on current grades
whUe higher priced coffees have suffered more
severely in many Instances.

EVERETT,. Wash., May 8.-Engineer
Michael J. Riley and Brakeman "A. L.
Kittle were killed this morning by an en
gine crashing through a trestle to the
bottom of a gulch twenty feet below. The
men were in the employ of the Snohomish
Logging Company. The engine fell upon
Riley, killing him instantly. Kittle died
soon after from scalds. Another train
which followed crashed through the same
trestle. The engineer and fireman es
caped by jumping.
Falling Engine Kills Two Men.

ing built on the midway for the perform
ance.
Bull-Fighting for Sacramento.
WASHINGTON, May 8.â The Interstate

the complaint of the Red Cloud Mining
Company against the Southern Pacific
Company, involving the freight tariff on
machinery from Erie, Pa:, to Saitoh, 'Cal.
The commission holds' that a contract
which the complainant claimed It made
with the, railroad for a charge upon the
shipment of machinery - lower I than/ the
published tariff was not -binding, -and its
violation furnished no grounds for re
dress. â¢ ...

GRASS VALLEY, May 8;â This city Is
wild with delight to-night over" the high
school movement carried at the polls, to
day by, an overwhelming majority. The
Outcome exceeded even the most -san
guine hopes of- the strongest friends of
the proposition. This was the result of
a well-planned campaign begun weeks
ago by friends of the proposed high
school. To-night a procession of business
men, school boys and others are parad
ing the streets, to thV beat of drums,
waving flags, cheering, ringing of bells
and firing of bombs and crackers.
Grass -Valley People Rejoice.

CHEYENNE, Wyo., May 8.-Mrs. An
drew T. Clark, wife of a well known con
tractor who lives in the northern part of
the City, while walking on the prairie
near her home' yesterday morning became
bewildered and i walked < twenty miles be
fore finding a human 'â¢habitation. She is
near-sighted, and when she Io3t sight of
the town started due north, although
thinking she had taken the opposite .di
rection. She spent the night on the open
prairie and this afternoon reached the
ranch of - O. H. Farthington on Pole
Creek, twenty miles from Cheyenne, in an
exhausted condition, having been thirty
hours without food. Search had been
made/ for her by. her friends .yesterday,
and -this morning a troop of cavalry and
nearly a hundred men started in search
and spent the- eritire day scouring the
plains in this vicinity.

I>ES MOINES, Iowa, May 8.â A wreck
occurred- on the Chicago and- Northwest
ern at Ames at 1 o'clock this morning. A
tramp was killed and Mail Clerk Griffin
of Des Moines was seriously Injured. Mail
Clerk Graves of Chicago was buried un
der mail sacks and badly bruised, and
Mail Clerk Alexander Turk of Chicago
had his hand and arm Injured. Train No.
10 was just approaching the town limits
from the west when the tender of the
engine, two mail cars and two express
cars left the track, plowing into the
gravel, but not overturning. The track
was tern up for a distance of 200 yards
and traffic is being delayed. The tramp
was riding on the front coach. Consider
able mail matter was water-soaked ana
destroyed. A soft roadbed is supposed to
have been responsible for the wreck.
Mail Clerks Are Seriously
Injured.

TACOMA, Wash:; May 8.â It is reported
from Skagway that large salmon com
panies are rapidly securing possession of
all the cannery sites on the Alaska
coast. The wonderful success which has
attendefl " the combinations of Puget
Sound and other canning interests have
resulted in causing something of a stam
pede to obtain available locations near
the mouth of Alaskan streams.
The Alaska Fisheries Union has ob
tained .of a large site near
Haines> at the mouth of Chilkat Inlet.
The company is preparing to build there
a cannery 80x440 feet in size and several
other Â¦ two-story buHdings.-' Eighty thou
sand dollars will be invented in the plant
before it starts. Surveyor Diers of
Skagway has just surveyed and staked
fourteen other cannery sites located at
favorable points between the head of
Lynn Canal and Wrangel for the same
concern. The eosajiany is comoosed of
Puget Sound capitalists, who aspire to
take a prominent place in the salmon
canning business. If the original can
nery at Haines proves successful some of
the other . sites will be speedily built
upon. King salmon are already running
In Alaskan waters.

CINCINNATI, May .'8.â Mrs. George W.
Herget was killed early to-day during a
carousal at her, home in Dayton, Ky. Her
husband; William Thornton, Frank Kent,
Thomas Shields and William '. Murray,
alias Ford Mattox, have been arrested on
the charge of murder. Herget is th^son
of a wealthy distiller, who died at Pe
king, 111., two years ago. Owing to his
habits his inheritance was left in trust.
When his monthly remittance came
Thornton, Kent, Shields and Murray were
accustomed to visit the Herget home. On
one of these occasions yesterday the ca
rousal continued during the night. At 1
o'clock this morning the officers were at
tracted by screaming, and found Mrs.
Herget lying prostrate in her yard,
stabbed. : She died aoon after reaching
the hospital without being able to make
a statement. The live men were taken
out of the house afterward in such condi
tion that none of them up to noon was
able to talk. The dead woman's maiden
name was Mary Sellinger or Clyde, Ohio.
They had no children.
His Friends Are Under
Arrest.

nese Inspector Lynch by Gin Wah, a Chi
nese. merchant at Sebastopol, Sonoma
County. A Chinese coolie, arrived several
months ago, claiming to be a native-born

Lynch went to Sebastopol and found that
Gin Wan kept a gambling house. Gin

preferred charges, of blackmail against
Lynch. . The Chinese Bureau- denied a
landing to Gin's putative son, but thÂ»

Department and -the coolie was allowed
to land as a native-born American citizen.

Or. the evening of October 18 last Wat
erson paid a visit to his old friend, Mrs.
Mary Flrnn, living on Dupont street,
near Vallejo. Waterson had been drink
ing heavily for several days previous and
on the night in question he Indulged to
such an extent that he could not return
home alone. Mrs. Flynn escorted him to
his room In the rear of his cobbling shop,
and after seeing him well provided for
Waterson is several years In advarce
of the scriptural allotment of three-score
and-ten. He kept a cobbling shop at 510
Post street for several years prior to Oc
tober 18, 1901, when he disappeared mys
teriously. He was well known to the
members of the Olympic Club, with
whom he did sufficient business to enable
him to lay by the tidy sum of $700 in the
Hibernla Bank. Waterson was a fat,
jolly old fellow, with a cheery greeting
for all, and a love for intoxicants, the
excessive gratification of which was re
sponsible for. the events which rendered
him dead to the world for a period of
six months, and who, until his accidental
identification on Wednesday, .was 'thought
to be snugly buried in Cypress Lawn
Cemetery. . : â¢. . .
A laughable feature of the affair, and
at the same time one that will perhaps
be officially investigated, is the" fart of
the filing by a physician of a bill against
the estate for services rendered. Water
son declaring he never saw the doctor in
question.
There was also a pathetic incident,
which now becomes ludicrous. A woman
to whom Waterson was devoted visited
the Morgrue and tearfully identified the
body there as his. .

Judge Murasky's court , to-day, -will, re
veal a peculiar story. 1 , of a man's disap
pearance, the finding I of his supposed
bod> In the bay, its burial and the subse
quent discovery that he is alive and the

. The police have In their i possession a
tintype of Hadley . dressed as a minister
which was taken at the Cliff House about
five years ago. ; None of Hadley's inti
mate friends ever knew him to essay the
role of a minister. -This is taken to indi
cate that Hadley was a man of pecu
liarly eccentric habitsâ just such a man
who would go about laying fiendish plans
for a. young girl's death in the stealthy
manner that the mysterious C. B. Haw
kins consummated his foul plot. .
Â¦Â¦"Â¦* "When ,the- time comes we hope -to pro
duce enough evidence to send Hadley to
the gallows," said Chief Wittman when
seen last night. "We are working away
on the case, getting a little information
every day. Captain Seymour has his best
detectives detailed on the case, and all
we want at this time Is the arrest of
Hadley. I feel confident that we will
have him in the toils before long. We
will follow him to the end of the earth,
if necessary, to bring him back to face
the charge of murder which the mother
of the dead girl has lodged against him."
DRESSED AS MINISTER.
Captain Seymour claims to have'dis
covered another ' strong: bit of evidence
which is damaging to Hadley. Captain
Seymour and his detectives- are conduct
ing the investigation quietly, and for rea
sons best known to themselves they In
tend to keep their discoveries secret foi
the time being.
Chief Wittman is in hopes that he will
receive a clew to Hadley's whereabouts
within the next few days. The fugitive
is well known throughout the big cities,
and it is quite likely that he will be rec
ognized through one of the photograpaic
circulars calling for his arrest which are
well distributed all over the country.
Both Captain Seymour and Chief WittÂ«
man have little faith in the theory that
Hadley has committed suicide. They are
convinced that he is in Hiding either in
Mexico or In some other neighboring for
eign territory. .
Much comment is made on the fact
that the two furniture men- who had
dealings with the mysterious Hawkins
and the grocery clerk, who saw him
with the fuller girl, identify the .photo
graph of Hadley so readily, while Krone,
the restaurant man. says he is not the
John Bennett who â¢ is supposed "to have
met Nora Fuller at the Popular Res
taurant the- day she disappeared. Krone
claimed to have known Hadley well, but
upon being questioned - closely â¢ he ad
mitted that he had not seen him- for
three years. This state of affairs, coupled
with the fact, that Krone said at the out
set-of the case that his patron, whom
he knew as John Bennett, sometimes
wore a clerical garb, and the police hav
ing established beyond a shadow of a
doubt that Hadley sometimes clad him
self. In ministerial garb, has given rise to
strange conjectures as to Krone's posi
tion.
Seldom in the history of crime ha3 a
police department penetrated into such a
deep mystery as that which surrounded
the Identity of the fiend who strangled
the unfortunate girl, and if Hadley is
caught and convicted of. the murder
much credit is due Chief of Police Witt
man and Chief of Detectives Seymour.
